Maybe we can compute the circumcircle of BEF using coordinates, and then compute the distance from O to L. Since we have numeric evidence, we can try to derive analytically.

Let's set up coordinate system: Let M=(0,0), N=(d,0), A=(a,b). We'll compute O = (Ox, Oy) as the circumcenter of B(a,-b), E, F. We can find O by solving the system: (O - B)·(E - B) = (O - B)·(F - B) = something? Actually the perpendicular bisector condition: |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2. So we have two equations: |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 and |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2. These are linear in O because the quadratic terms cancel. So we get:

2 O·(E-B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2,
2 O·(F-B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

So O is determined by solving two linear equations. This might be tractable.

Let’s compute E and F in terms of A and P. We have:

E = (1 - λ) A + λ P, with λ = rT/|v|^2.
F = (1 - μ) A + μ P, with μ = RT/|v|^2.

Also, note that |v|^2 = |P-A|^2 = (P-A)·(P-A) = |P|^2 + |A|^2 - 2 A·P. But we have expressions for A·P, etc.

We can compute |E|^2, |F|^2, and also E-B, F-B. Since B = R_x(A) = (a,-b). In terms of vectors, if we denote the reflection across x-axis as R, then R(A)=B, R(P)=P' (since P=(p,y_P), R(P)=(p,-y_P)). Also, note that v = P-A, so R(v) = P' - B.

Now we have E = A + λ v, so R(E) = R(A) + λ R(v) = B + λ (P'-B). That's exactly the point on line BP' with same parameter λ. So E' = B + λ (P'-B) is the reflection of E. Similarly, F' = B + μ (P'-B) is the reflection of F. So indeed, the points on line BP' correspond to the reflected points.

Now we need to solve for O from the equations:

2 O·(E-B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2,
2 O·(F-B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

Let’s denote:

E - B = (A + λ v) - B = (A - B) + λ v.
But A - B = (a,b) - (a,-b) = (0, 2b) = 2b e2. So A-B = 2b e2. v = v_x e1 + v_y e2.

So E-B = 2b e2 + λ (v_x e1 + v_y e2) = λ v_x e1 + (2b + λ v_y) e2.

Similarly, F-B = μ v_x e1 + (2b + μ v_y) e2.

Now |E|^2 = |A + λ v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2λ A·v + λ^2 |v|^2 = r^2 + 2λ A·v + λ^2 |v|^2.
|B|^2 = |A|^2? Actually |B|^2 = |A|^2 = r^2, since B is reflection of A. So |B|^2 = r^2.

Thus |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 2λ A·v + λ^2 |v|^2.

But we have A·v = -r(p+r) (from earlier). And |v|^2 = v_x^2+v_y^2. Also, λ = rT/|v|^2. So:

2λ A·v = 2 (rT/|v|^2) (-r(p+r)) = -2r^2 T (p+r)/|v|^2.
λ^2 |v|^2 = (r^2 T^2/|v|^4) * |v|^2 = r^2 T^2/|v|^2.

So |E|^2 - |B|^2 = (1/|v|^2) ( -2r^2 T (p+r) + r^2 T^2 ) = r^2 T ( -2(p+r) + T ) / |v|^2.

But T = d+R+r. Also, p+r = (d+R-r)/2 + r = (d+R+r)/2 = T/2. So p+r = T/2. Then -2(p+r) = -T. So -2(p+r) + T = -T + T = 0. Thus |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 0! That's interesting: |E|^2 = |B|^2 = r^2. So E lies on the same circle centered at M as B? Indeed, E is on Ω, so |E|=r, and B is also on Ω, so yes |E|=|B|=r. So |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 0. That means the equation 2 O·(E-B) = 0, so O·(E-B)=0. So O is perpendicular to E-B. Similarly, we can compute |F|^2 - |B|^2. F is on Γ, so |F-d| = R. But |B|^2 = r^2. So |F|^2 =? Not necessarily equal to r^2. Let's compute:

|F|^2 = |A + μ v|^2 = r^2 + 2μ A·v + μ^2 |v|^2.
μ = R T/|v|^2.
So 2μ A·v = 2 (R T/|v|^2) (-r(p+r)) = -2R r T (p+r)/|v|^2 = -2R r T (T/2)/|v|^2 = -R r T^2/|v|^2.
μ^2 |v|^2 = (R^2 T^2/|v|^4)*|v|^2 = R^2 T^2/|v|^2.
Thus |F|^2 - r^2 = (1/|v|^2)( -R r T^2 + R^2 T^2 ) = R T^2 (R - r)/|v|^2.

Now note that R - r =? From earlier, R^2 - r^2 = d(d-2a) = (R-r)(R+r) = d(d-2a). So R - r = d(d-2a)/(R+r). That is positive. So |F|^2 - |B|^2 = R T^2 (R - r)/|v|^2. That is not zero generally. So the second equation gives 2 O·(F-B) = |F|^2 - r^2. So O·(F-B) = (|F|^2 - r^2)/2.

Now we have O·(E-B)=0. So O is orthogonal to E-B. Also, O·(F-B) is known. Since E-B and F-B are known vectors, we can solve for O.

Now we also have that O is the circumcenter of triangle BEF, so we could also compute O using the perpendicular bisector of B and F? But we already have the linear equations.

Now we need to find O in coordinates. Let's denote:

E-B = α e1 + β e2, where α = λ v_x, β = 2b + λ v_y.
F-B = γ e1 + δ e2, where γ = μ v_x, δ = 2b + μ v_y.

We have O·(E-B)=0, and O·(F-B)= (|F|^2 - r^2)/2.

Let O = (Ox, Oy). Then:

α Ox + β Oy = 0,
γ Ox + δ Oy = (|F|^2 - r^2)/2.

So we can solve for Ox, Oy. This gives:

Ox = -β * (|F|^2 - r^2)/(2(αδ - βγ))? Actually solve linear system:

[α β; γ δ] [Ox; Oy] = [0; K], where K = (|F|^2 - r^2)/2.

Then using Cramer's rule: Ox = det([0 β; K δ]) / det([α β; γ δ]) = (0*δ - β*K) / (αδ - βγ) = -βK / (αδ - βγ). Oy = det([α 0; γ K]) / (αδ - βγ) = (αK - 0*γ) / (αδ - βγ) = αK / (αδ - βγ).

So O = ( -βK/(Δ), αK/Δ ), where Δ = αδ - βγ.

Now we have O coordinates expressed in terms of α,β,γ,δ, and K. Then we can compute the distance from O to line L, which is line through H = (p, y_H) with direction v. But we also have that L is parallel to v, so its normal vector is perpendicular to v. We can compute distance from O to L as |(O - H) · n| / |n|, where n is a normal to v. Choose n = (v_y, -v_x) = (v_y, -v_x). Then distance d(O,L) = |(O - H)·(v_y, -v_x)| / sqrt(v_x^2+v_y^2) = |(O_x - p) v_y + (O_y - y_H) (-v_x)| / |v| = |(O_x - p) v_y - (O_y - y_H) v_x| / |v|.

So = (1/2)[ (1+R-r)(R-r)T + (1 - R + r)(T-2a) ].

Now we also have the relation between a and R,r. From (A): 2a = r^2+1-R^2. So a = (r^2+1-R^2)/2. Then T-2a = (1+R+r) - (r^2+1-R^2) = R+r - r^2 + R^2 = (R^2+R) - (r^2 - r) = (R(R+1)) - (r(r-1))? Actually r^2 - r = r(r-1). So T-2a = R(R+1) - r(r-1). But note that R>r, so this is positive. But maybe we can also express it as (R-r)(R+r+1) - (r^2 - r)? Not.

Alternatively, we might compute (O-H)·v = (R T)/2 - (p v_x + y_H v_y) = (R T)/2 - (1/2)[ p(R-r)T + (1-p)(T-2a) ] = (1/2)[ R T - p(R-r)T - (1-p)(T-2a) ] = (1/2)[ R T - p(R-r)T - (1-p)(T-2a) ].

Now note that T = 1+R+r. So R T = R(1+R+r) = R + R^2 + Rr. And p(R-r)T = p(R-r)(1+R+r). That seems messy.

Maybe we can find a simpler way: Since we have O·v = (R T)/2, and we have H = (p, y_H). Also, we have that H is on the altitude from P, so x-coordinate p. Also, note that A = (a,b). And v = P-A. Let's try to compute O×v using the expression for O in terms of u_perp. We have O = (K/Δ) u_perp, with u_perp = ( -(2b+λ v_y), λ v_x ). Then O×v = O_x v_y - O_y v_x = (K/Δ)[ -(2b+λ v_y) v_y - λ v_x v_x ] = (K/Δ)[ - (2b+λ v_y) v_y - λ v_x^2 ] = -(K/Δ)[ (2b+λ v_y) v_y + λ v_x^2 ] = -(K/Δ)[ 2b v_y + λ v_y^2 + λ v_x^2 ] = -(K/Δ)[ 2b v_y + λ |v|^2 ].

Now λ |v|^2 = rT. So O×v = -(K/Δ)(2b v_y + rT). But we also have Δ = -2b v_x (R-r)T/|v|^2. So:

O×v = -(K) * (2b v_y + rT) / ( -2b v_x (R-r)T/|v|^2 ) = (K (2b v_y + rT) |v|^2) / (2b v_x (R-r)T).

Now K = R T^2 (R-r)/(2|v|^2). So:

O×v = [R T^2 (R-r)/(2|v|^2)] * (2b v_y + rT) |v|^2 / (2b v_x (R-r)T) = R T^2 (R-r) (2b v_y + rT) / (2 * 2b v_x (R-r)T) = R T (2b v_y + rT) / (4b v_x).

Thus O×v = (R T (2b v_y + rT))/(4b v_x). Now note that v_x = (R-r)T/2. So:

O×v = (R T (2b v_y + rT))/(4b * ((R-r)T/2)) = (R T (2b v_y + rT) * 2)/(4b (R-r)T) = (R (2b v_y + rT))/(2b (R-r)). So:

O×v = (R (2b v_y + rT))/(2b (R-r)).   (H)

Now also, O·v = (R T)/2. So we have O expressed in terms of v and something. But we need (O-H)×v = O×v - H×v. Since H = (p, y_H). Compute H×v = p v_y + y_H (-v_x)? Actually cross product in 2D: (x1,y1)×(x2,y2) = x1 y2 - y1 x2. So H×v = p v_y - y_H v_x. So:

(O-H)×v = O×v - (p v_y - y_H v_x) = O×v - p v_y + y_H v_x.

Thus the distance from O to L is |(O-H)×v|/|v| = |O×v - p v_y + y_H v_x|/|v|.

Given the complexity, maybe we can find a synthetic proof that is more elegant. I recall a known problem: "Two circles intersect at A and B. The line through the centers meets the circles at C and D. P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD. AP meets the circles again at E and F. H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." This might be a problem from the 2011 IMO Shortlist? Possibly Geometry problem. There might be a solution using the concept of the Miquel point or spiral similarity. Let's try to think synthetically.

Given circles Ω and Γ intersect at A and B. The line MN (centers) meets Ω at C and Γ at D. So C is on Ω, D on Γ. The circumcenter P of ACD. Since C and D are on the line of centers, the circle ACD is such that its center P lies on the perpendicular bisector of CD. Also, note that M is the center of Ω, so MC = r. Similarly, ND = R. Since C, M, N, D are collinear in that order, we have CM = r, MN = d, ND = R. So CD = CM + MN + ND = r + d + R.

Now consider the spiral similarity that sends segment AC to AD? Not.

Maybe we can consider the circle with diameter CD. Its center is the midpoint of CD, call it O_CD = (p,0). The circle with diameter CD has radius (r+d+R)/2. Also, note that the circle with diameter CD passes through C and D, but not necessarily through A. However, the power of A with respect to that circle is: A's power = |A-O_CD|^2 - ((r+d+R)/2)^2. Since A is on Ω, we have |A-M|^2 = r^2. M = (0,0). So |A-O_CD|^2 = (a-p)^2 + (b-0)^2 = (a-p)^2 + b^2. But we might compute that.

The product of distances from A to the intersections with Ω is something? Actually for a line through A, the second intersection E with Ω satisfies that AE is the chord of Ω. Maybe we can use the fact that P is the circumcenter of ACD, so AP is the line from A to the center of circle through A, C, D. So AP is a radius line. The reflection of C across AP? Not.

Let's try to find relationships with the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical line x=p. So H lies on that line. Also, the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we have that HM is perpendicular to PN. That is, (H - M) · (P - N) = 0. Since M=(0,0), H=(p, y_H), so (p, y_H)·(P - N) = 0. But P = (p, y_P), N = (1,0) (since d=1). So P - N = (p-1, y_P). So p(p-1) + y_H y_P = 0 => y_H = -p(p-1)/y_P. That's consistent with earlier.

Now line through H parallel to AP: that line L is such that its direction is v = P - A. So L is the translation of line AP by vector H - A. So L is the line through H with same direction. So L is the image of AP under the translation that sends A to H. That translation also sends P to H + (P-A) = H + v. So the translation sends the line AP to line through H parallel to AP. That's L. So L is the line through H that is the translation of AP by vector H-A.

Now consider the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Since B is the reflection of A across MN (x-axis), and E and F are on line AP, maybe the circle (BEF) is the reflection of the circle through A and the reflections of E and F? That is, reflect the whole configuration across x-axis: A becomes B, P becomes P' (since P is at (p, y_P), its reflection is (p, -y_P) = P' (which is above x-axis). The line AP becomes the line through B and P'. The points E and F on AP become points E' and F' on BP'. So the circle (BEF) reflects to the circle (A E' F'). So if we can show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF), then after reflection, the line through H' parallel to BP' is tangent to circle (A E' F'). But H' = (p, -y_H). Since y_H negative, -y_H positive. So H' is above x-axis. The line through H' parallel to BP' is the reflection of L. So the claim is equivalent to: In the reflected configuration, the line through H' parallel to BP' is tangent to circle (A E' F'). Now note that in the reflected configuration, we have triangle B'? Not.

Maybe we can find that the circle (A E' F') is the circumcircle of triangle A and the two points where the line through P' parallel to something? Not.

Given the difficulty, perhaps the analytic proof is the most straightforward. We already derived many simplifications. Let's continue with the analytic proof using d=1. We have expressions for O, H, v, etc. We need to show that the distance from O to L equals the circumradius. Since we have O·v = (R T)/2, and we have H·v = p v_x + y_H v_y. Let's compute H·v.

We have v = (v_x, v_y). H = (p, y_H). So H·v = p v_x + y_H v_y.

We computed p v_x = p * (R-r)T/2 = ( (1+R-r)/2 ) * (R-r)T/2 = (1+R-r)(R-r)T/4. And y_H v_y = (1-p)(T-2a)/2. So:

Now compute (O-H)·v = O·v - H·v = (R T)/2 - (1/2)[ p(R-r)T + (1-p)(T-2a) ] = (1/2)[ R T - p(R-r)T - (1-p)(T-2a) ].

We can try to simplify the bracket: R T - p(R-r)T - (1-p)(T-2a) = T(R - p(R-r)) - (1-p)(T-2a). But p = (1+R-r)/2, so p(R-r) = (1+R-r)(R-r)/2 = ((R-r) + (R-r)^2)/2. Then R - p(R-r) = R - ((R-r)+(R-r)^2)/2 = (2R - (R-r) - (R-r)^2)/2 = (2R - R + r - (R^2 -2Rr + r^2))/2 = (R + r - R^2 +2Rr - r^2)/2 = ( -R^2 +3Rr - r^2 + R + r )/2. That doesn't look nice.

Maybe we can find a better expression for H·v using geometry: Since H is orthocenter, we have that HM is perpendicular to PN, and HN is perpendicular to PM. So H·(P-N) = 0? Actually (H-M)·(P-N)=0 gives H·(P-N)=0. But M=0. So H·(P-N)=0 => H·P = H·N. Since N=(1,0), H·N = p*1 + y_H*0 = p. So H·P = p. But P = (p, y_P), so H·P = p*p + y_H y_P = p^2 + y_H y_P. But y_P negative, so y_H negative. From H·P = p, we get p^2 + y_H y_P = p => y_H y_P = p - p^2 = p(1-p). So y_H = p(1-p)/y_P. Since y_P negative, y_H negative. Good.

Now we also have that H·(P-N)=0 => H·P = H·N => H·P = p. So that's the same.

Now maybe we can compute H·v using that v = P-A. So H·v = H·P - H·A = p - H·A. So if we can compute H·A, then H·v = p - H·A. And then (O-H)·v = O·v - (p - H·A) = (R T)/2 - p + H·A = H·A - p + (R T)/2.

Now we need to compute H·A. H = (p, y_H), A = (a,b). So H·A = p a + y_H b. We have y_H = p(1-p)/y_P, and y_P = -p(r+a)/b. So y_H = p(1-p) / (-p(r+a)/b) = -b(1-p)/(r+a). Thus y_H b = -b^2(1-p)/(r+a). So H·A = p a - b^2(1-p)/(r+a). But b^2 = r^2 - a^2. So:

H·A = p a - (r^2 - a^2)(1-p)/(r+a) = p a - ( (r-a)(r+a)(1-p)/(r+a) ) = p a - (r-a)(1-p) = p a - (1-p)r + (1-p)a = p a + a(1-p) - (1-p)r = a(p+1-p) - (1-p)r = a - (1-p)r.

Thus H·A = a - r(1-p). Great! So H·A = a - r(1-p). Then H·v = p - H·A = p - a + r(1-p) = (p-a) + r(1-p). But v_x = p - a. So H·v = v_x + r(1-p).

Now compute (O-H)·v = O·v - H·v = (R T)/2 - (v_x + r(1-p)) = (R T)/2 - v_x - r(1-p). But v_x = (R-r)T/2. So (O-H)·v = (R T)/2 - (R-r)T/2 - r(1-p) = (R T - (R-r)T)/2 - r(1-p) = (r T)/2 - r(1-p) = r( T/2 - (1-p) ). So:

(O-H)·v = r( T/2 - (1-p) ).   (I)

|O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v)^2/|v|^2 = |O-B|^2.

This is equivalent to:

|O-H|^2 - |O-B|^2 = ((O-H)·v)^2)/|v|^2.

But the left side is something like? We can compute |O-H|^2 - |O-B|^2 = (|O|^2 + |H|^2 - 2O·H) - (|O|^2 + |B|^2 - 2O·B) = |H|^2 - |B|^2 - 2O·(H-B). So we need to show:

|H|^2 - |B|^2 - 2O·(H-B) = ((O-H)·v)^2/|v|^2.

Given the time, perhaps we can adopt a different approach: Use coordinates but with a more convenient choice. For instance, place the common chord AB vertically, and let the centers M and N lie on the x-axis. That's what we did. But we can also set the circle Ω to be centered at (0,0) with radius 1 (by scaling). That's fine: set r=1. Then the circle Ω is unit circle. Then A and B are points on unit circle symmetric about x-axis. So A = (cosθ, sinθ), B = (cosθ, -sinθ) with sinθ>0. Then the center N is such that NA = R, and the distance between M and N is d = MN. Since we have M=(0,0), N=(d,0). Then R = distance from N to A = sqrt((d - cosθ)^2 + sin^2θ) = sqrt(d^2 -2d cosθ +1). Also, we have r=1, so r<R implies 1 < sqrt(d^2 -2d cosθ +1) => d^2 -2d cosθ >0 => d(d-2cosθ)>0 => since d>0, d>2cosθ. Also, the circles intersect at two distinct points, so we need |R-1| < d < R+1. Now P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. A=(cosθ, sinθ), C=(-1,0), D=(d+R,0). Then we can compute P. Expand: P_x^2+2P_x+1 + P_y^2 = P_x^2 -2P_x cosθ + cos^2θ + P_y^2 -2P_y sinθ + sin^2θ. Cancel P_x^2, P_y^2: 2P_x+1 = -2P_x cosθ + cos^2θ+ sin^2θ -2P_y sinθ = -2P_x cosθ +1 -2P_y sinθ. So 2P_x = -2P_x cosθ -2P_y sinθ => divide 2: P_x = -P_x cosθ - P_y sinθ => P_x(1+cosθ) = -P_y sinθ => P_y = -P_x (1+cosθ)/ sinθ. Since sinθ>0, P_y negative. So P = ( (d+R-1)/2 , - (d+R-1)(1+cosθ)/(2 sinθ) ). Good.

Given the time, perhaps we can produce a solution using complex numbers with the unit circle, and use known formulas for circumcenter and orthocenter. Let's try that.

Set M=0, N=d (real), and A = e^{iα} (on unit circle, α∈(0,π)), so A = e^{iα} = cosα + i sinα. Then B = e^{-iα}. Then R = |A-d| = |e^{iα} - d| = sqrt(d^2 - 2d cosα +1). Let T = d + R + 1. Then P = circumcenter of A, C=-1, D=d+R. Since C and D are real, P = (p, y_P) where p = (d+R-1)/2, and y_P = -p(1+cosα)/sinα. In complex form, P = p + i y_P.

Now line AP: direction v = P - A. Then E = A + t_E v, with t_E = -2 Re(A * conj(v))/|v|^2? Actually in complex numbers, if we have a line through A with direction w, then the other intersection with unit circle is given by: E = (A - w^2/ (2 A) )? Not.

Better: For a point A on unit circle, the second intersection of line through A with direction w is given by: E = (A - w^2/ (2 A) )? Let's derive: Param: A + t w. Condition |A + t w| = 1. Since |A|=1, we have 2 Re(A conj(t w)) + |t w|^2 = 0 => 2t Re(A conj(w)) + t^2 |w|^2 =0 => t (2 Re(A conj(w)) + t |w|^2)=0. So t=0 or t = -2 Re(A conj(w))/|w|^2. So E = A + t w = A - 2 Re(A conj(w))/|w|^2 * w. In complex numbers, Re(A conj(w)) = (A conj(w) + conj(A) w)/2. So t = - (A conj(w) + conj(A) w)/|w|^2. Then E = A - (A conj(w) + conj(A) w)/|w|^2 * w. This is not a simple complex formula.

But we might use the fact that for a line through A, the other intersection is the inversion of A in the line? Not.

Given the difficulty of synthetic, maybe the analytic approach is the most feasible to produce a rigorous proof. Let's try to compute the condition for tangency using the expressions we have for O, H, v, etc., but perhaps we can find that the distance from O to L equals |O-B| by showing that the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) is equal to something like (H-B)^2? Maybe we can compute |H-O|^2 - |O-B|^2 = (H-O)·(H-O) - (B-O)·(B-O) = (H·H -2H·O + O·O) - (B·B -2B·O + O·O) = |H|^2 - |B|^2 - 2O·(H-B). So that's equal to |H|^2 - |B|^2 - 2O·(H-B). And we also have (O-H)·v =? Not directly.

Another angle: Since L is parallel to v, the distance from O to L is the absolute value of the component of (O-H) perpendicular to v. That distance squared = |O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v)^2/|v|^2. So we need to prove:

F = A + (R T/|v|^2) v.

Now we need to prove the tangency condition. We have B = (cosθ, -sinθ). We have O is the circumcenter of B, E, F. We can compute O using the vector formulas we had. Since we have expressions for O in terms of v_x, v_y, and constants, we can try to compute the distance from O to line L.

We have O satisfies: 2s Oy + (T/|v|^2) D = 0 (from O·(E-B)=0) and 2s Oy + (R T/|v|^2) D = K (from O·(F-B)=K). We already solved for Oy and D. We found:

D = d R v_x/(R-1). (From earlier: D = d R v_x/(R-1))
Oy = K/(2s(1-R)) = - (d R T v_x)/(2s (R-1) |v|^2)? Actually K = d R T v_x/|v|^2. So Oy = (d R T v_x/|v|^2) / (2s(1-R)) = - (d R T v_x)/(2s (R-1) |v|^2). So that's Oy.

Now we also have Ox = (D - Oy v_y)/v_x. Since D = d R v_x/(R-1), and Oy v_y = - (d R T v_x)/(2s (R-1) |v|^2) * v_y. v_y = - (1+c) T/(2s). So Oy v_y = - (d R T v_x)/(2s (R-1) |v|^2) * (- (1+c) T/(2s)) = (d R T v_x (1+c) T)/(4s^2 (R-1) |v|^2) = (d R v_x (1+c) T^2)/(4s^2 (R-1) |v|^2). Then D - Oy v_y = d R v_x/(R-1) - (d R v_x (1+c) T^2)/(4s^2 (R-1) |v|^2) = d R v_x/(R-1) [1 - ( (1+c) T^2)/(4s^2 |v|^2) ]. Then Ox = (D - Oy v_y)/v_x = d R/(R-1) [1 - ( (1+c) T^2)/(4s^2 |v|^2) ].

Thus O = (Ox, Oy) with:

Ox = d R/(R-1) [1 - ( (1+c) T^2)/(4s^2 |v|^2) ],
Oy = - (d R T v_x)/(2s (R-1) |v|^2).

Now we need to compute (O-H)×v. H = (p, y_H) with p = (d+R-1)/2, y_H = -s (d+1-R)/(2(1+c)). But we can also write y_H = -s ( (d+1) - R )/(2(1+c)). And v = (v_x, v_y) with v_x = (U-1)/2, v_y = - (1+c) T/(2s). Also, note that |v|^2 = v_x^2+v_y^2.

Now compute O-H = (Ox-p, Oy-y_H). Then C = (Ox-p) v_y - (Oy-y_H) v_x.

We can try to compute C using the expressions we have. We already have an expression for C in terms of T/s and v_x, etc., which we derived earlier: C = (T/s) B1 + B2. But we can also compute directly using the simplified forms.

Let's compute C = (O-H)×v. We'll compute each term.

First, compute Ox-p. Ox = d R/(R-1) [1 - ( (1+c) T^2/(4s^2 |v|^2) ]. p = (d+R-1)/2.

We can also express |v|^2 in terms of d, c, s, R, T. We have v_x = (d+R-1-2c)/2 = (U-1)/2, v_y = - (1+c) T/(2s). So |v|^2 = ((U-1)^2)/4 + (1+c)^2 T^2/(4 s^2) = (1/4)[(U-1)^2 + (1+c)^2 T^2/s^2]. Now U = d+R-2c. So U-1 = d+R-2c-1 = 2p-2c? Actually p = (d+R-1)/2, so 2p = d+R-1, so U-1 = (d+R-1)-2c = 2p-2c = 2(p-c) = 2 v_x. So (U-1)^2 = 4 v_x^2. So |v|^2 = (1/4)[4 v_x^2 + (1+c)^2 T^2/s^2] = v_x^2 + (1+c)^2 T^2/(4 s^2). So that's consistent.

Now compute (1+c) T^2/(4s^2 |v|^2). That is a constant. Let’s denote α = (1+c) T^2/(4s^2 |v|^2). Then Ox = d R/(R-1) (1 - α). So Ox = d R/(R-1) - (d R/(R-1)) α.

Now p = (d+R-1)/2. We can also express p in terms of d, R, and maybe T. Since T = d+R+1, we have p = (d+R-1)/2 = (T-2)/2? Actually T = d+R+1, so T-2 = d+R-1. So p = (T-2)/2. So p = T/2 - 1. So Ox-p = d R/(R-1) - (d R/(R-1)) α - (T/2 - 1).

Now compute Oy. Oy = - (d R T v_x)/(2s (R-1) |v|^2). Note that v_x = (U-1)/2 = (d+R-1-2c)/2 = (2p-2c)/2 = p-c. So v_x = p-c. And |v|^2 = v_x^2 + (1+c)^2 T^2/(4 s^2). So we can write Oy = - (d R T (p-c))/(2s (R-1) |v|^2).

Now y_H = -s (d+1-R)/(2(1+c)). So Oy - y_H = - (d R T (p-c))/(2s (R-1) |v|^2) + s (d+1-R)/(2(1+c)).

Now C = (Ox-p) v_y - (Oy-y_H) v_x.

v_y = - (1+c) T/(2s). So (Ox-p) v_y = (Ox-p) * (- (1+c) T/(2s)) = - (1+c) T/(2s) (Ox-p).

Now (Oy-y_H) v_x = v_x * (Oy-y_H). So:

C = - (1+c) T/(2s) (Ox-p) - v_x (Oy-y_H).

Now substitute Ox-p and Oy-y_H.

C = - (1+c) T/(2s) [ d R/(R-1) - (d R/(R-1)) α - (T/2 - 1) ] - v_x [ - (d R T (p-c))/(2s (R-1) |v|^2) + s (d+1-R)/(2(1+c)) ].

Simplify the first part: - (1+c) T/(2s) * d R/(R-1) + (1+c) T/(2s) * (d R/(R-1)) α + (1+c) T/(2s) * (T/2 - 1).

Now the second part: - v_x * ( - (d R T (p-c))/(2s (R-1) |v|^2) + s (d+1-R)/(2(1+c)) ) = + v_x * (d R T (p-c))/(2s (R-1) |v|^2) - v_x * s (d+1-R)/(2(1+c)).

Given the time, I'll produce an analytic proof using coordinates and then simplify using the derived identities. I'll try to present it as a series of algebraic steps that lead to the conclusion. Since the user expects an improved proof, I'll produce a rigorous proof using analytic geometry, but I'll try to keep it organized.

I'll set up coordinates: M=(0,0), N=(d,0). Let the circles intersect at A=(a,b) and B=(a,-b) with b>0. Without loss of generality, we can scale so that the radius of Ω is 1, i.e., a^2+b^2=1. So we set a=cosθ, b=sinθ, with 0<θ<π/2 (since we need r<R, which gives cosθ>0). Then R = sqrt((cosθ-d)^2+sin^2θ) = sqrt(d^2-2d cosθ+1). The points C and D are C=(-1,0), D=(d+R,0). The circumcenter P of triangle ACD: since C and D are on x-axis, P lies on the perpendicular bisector of CD, which is the vertical line x = (C_x+D_x)/2 = (-1+d+R)/2 = (d+R-1)/2. So P_x = p = (d+R-1)/2. The condition PA=PC gives P_y = -p(1+cosθ)/sinθ. So P = (p, -p(1+cosθ)/sinθ).

Now line AP: direction v = (p - cosθ, -p(1+cosθ)/sinθ - sinθ). Compute v_y = -(p(1+cosθ)+sin^2θ)/sinθ = -(1+cosθ)(p+1-cosθ)/sinθ. So v = (p - cosθ, -(1+cosθ)(p+1-cosθ)/sinθ).

Let U = d+R-2cosθ. Then p - cosθ = (d+R-1)/2 - cosθ = (d+R-1-2cosθ)/2 = (U-1)/2. And p+1-cosθ = (d+R-1)/2 +1 - cosθ = (d+R+1)/2 - cosθ = T/2 - cosθ, where T = d+R+1. So v = ((U-1)/2, -(1+cosθ)(T/2 - cosθ)/sinθ).

Now E is the second intersection of AP with Ω (circle centered at M with radius 1). Using the parameter t satisfying |A+tv|^2=1, we get t_E = -2(A·v)/|v|^2 = 2(p+1)/|v|^2 = T/|v|^2. So E = A + (T/|v|^2) v.

Similarly, F is the second intersection of AP with Γ (circle centered at N with radius R). Using the parameter t satisfying |A+tv-d|^2=R^2, we get t_F = -2((A-d)·v)/|v|^2 = R T/|v|^2. So F = A + (R T/|v|^2) v.

Now define B = (cosθ, -sinθ).

We need to show that the line through H parallel to v is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF. H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical, so H lies on the line x = p. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. The vector PN = N-P = (d-p, -P_y). The line through M perpendicular to PN is given by param: λ (P_y, d-p) (since (P_y, d-p) dot (d-p, -P_y)=0). The intersection with x=p gives λ P_y = p => λ = p/P_y. Then y_H = λ (d-p) = p(d-p)/P_y. So H = (p, p(d-p)/P_y). Since P_y = -p(1+cosθ)/sinθ, we have y_H = p(d-p) / (-p(1+cosθ)/sinθ) = - (d-p) sinθ/(1+cosθ). So H = (p, - (d-p) sinθ/(1+cosθ)).

Now we need to find the circumcircle of B, E, F. Let O be its circumcenter. We can find O by solving the linear system from O·(E-B)=0 and O·(F-B)=K, where K = (|F|^2-1)/2 (since |B|=1). Compute A·v = - (p+1) = -T/2. So t_E = T/|v|^2. Then E-B = A - B + t_E v = (cosθ - cosθ, sinθ - (-sinθ)) + t_E v = (0, 2 sinθ) + t_E v. So u = E-B = (t_E v_x, 2 sinθ + t_E v_y). Similarly, F-B = (t_F v_x, 2 sinθ + t_F v_y). The condition O·u=0 gives:

Ox t_E v_x + Oy (2 sinθ + t_E v_y) = 0.   (1)

Condition O·(F-B)=K gives:

Ox t_F v_x + Oy (2 sinθ + t_F v_y) = K.   (2)

Now t_F = R T/|v|^2, t_E = T/|v|^2. So t_F = R t_E. So we have:

t_E (Ox v_x + Oy v_y) + 2 sinθ Oy = 0 => t_E D + 2 sinθ Oy = 0, where D = O·v = Ox v_x + Oy v_y.
t_F D + 2 sinθ Oy = K => R t_E D + 2 sinθ Oy = K.

Thus we have:

t_E D + 2 sinθ Oy = 0,
R t_E D + 2 sinθ Oy = K.

Subtract: (R-1) t_E D = K => D = K/((R-1) t_E) = K |v|^2/((R-1) T). Since K = (|F|^2-1)/2, and we derived earlier that |F|^2 = 1 + 2d μ v_x with μ = t_F = R T/|v|^2, so |F|^2 = 1 + 2d (R T/|v|^2) v_x = 1 + 2d R T v_x/|v|^2. Thus K = d R T v_x/|v|^2. So D = (d R T v_x/|v|^2) * |v|^2/((R-1) T) = d R v_x/(R-1). So D = d R v_x/(R-1). (Equation for D)

Then from t_E D + 2 sinθ Oy = 0, we get Oy = - t_E D/(2 sinθ) = - (T/|v|^2) * (d R v_x/(R-1)) / (2 sinθ) = - (d R T v_x)/(2 sinθ (R-1) |v|^2). So Oy = - (d R T v_x)/(2 sinθ (R-1) |v|^2).

Now from (1) we can also find Ox: t_E (Ox v_x + Oy v_y) + 2 sinθ Oy = 0 => t_E Ox v_x + t_E Oy v_y + 2 sinθ Oy = 0 => Ox v_x = - (t_E Oy v_y + 2 sinθ Oy)/t_E = - ( (t_E v_y + 2 sinθ) Oy / t_E? Actually careful: t_E Ox v_x + t_E Oy v_y + 2 sinθ Oy = 0 => Ox v_x = - (t_E Oy v_y + 2 sinθ Oy)/t_E = - (t_E v_y + 2 sinθ) Oy / t_E. But we have an expression for Oy, so Ox = - (t_E v_y + 2 sinθ) Oy / (t_E v_x). This might be simplified.

Alternatively, we can use the expression for Ox from the earlier derived formula: Ox = d R/(R-1) [1 - ( (1+cosθ) T^2)/(4 sin^2θ |v|^2) ]. That formula was derived from the conditions. So we can use that.

Now we have O = (Ox, Oy) as above. Then we need to compute the distance from O to line L: H + t v. The distance is |(O-H)×v|/|v|. Let’s compute C = (O-H)×v.

We have O-H = (Ox - p, Oy - y_H). v = (v_x, v_y). So C = (Ox - p) v_y - (Oy - y_H) v_x.

Now we also need the circumradius R_circ = |O-B|. We can compute |O-B|^2.

We will now compute both sides and show they are equal. This will involve heavy algebra, but we can try to simplify using the relations.

First, compute v_x, v_y, p, y_H in terms of d, θ, R, T.

We have:

c = cosθ, s = sinθ.
R^2 = d^2 - 2d c + 1.
T = d + R + 1.
p = (d+R-1)/2.
v_x = p - c = (d+R-1)/2 - c = (d+R-1-2c)/2.
v_y = - (1+c)(p+1-c)/s = - (1+c)(T/2 - c)/s.
Also, y_H = - (d-p) s/(1+c) = - (d - (d+R-1)/2) s/(1+c) = - ((2d - d - R +1)/2) s/(1+c) = - ((d - R +1)/2) s/(1+c) = - (d+1-R) s/(2(1+c)).

Now note that (d+1-R) = T - 2c - 2? Actually T = d+R+1, so d+1-R = T - 2R? Not. But we can keep.

Now we have D = O·v = d R v_x/(R-1). And Oy = - (d R T v_x)/(2 s (R-1) |v|^2). Also, Ox = d R/(R-1) (1 - α) with α = (1+c) T^2/(4 s^2 |v|^2). So Ox = d R/(R-1) - (d R/(R-1)) α.

Now compute Ox - p = d R/(R-1) - (d R/(R-1)) α - (T/2 - 1) (since p = T/2 - 1). So Ox - p = d R/(R-1) - (d R/(R-1)) α - (T/2 - 1).

Now compute Oy - y_H = - (d R T v_x)/(2 s (R-1) |v|^2) + (d+1-R) s/(2(1+c)). Since y_H = - (d+1-R)s/(2(1+c)). So Oy - y_H = - (d R T v_x)/(2 s (R-1) |v|^2) + (d+1-R)s/(2(1+c)).

Now compute C = (Ox-p) v_y - (Oy-y_H) v_x.

We'll compute each term.

First, (Ox-p) v_y = [d R/(R-1) - (d R/(R-1)) α - (T/2 - 1)] * v_y.

Second, (Oy-y_H) v_x = [ - (d R T v_x)/(2 s (R-1) |v|^2) + (d+1-R)s/(2(1+c)) ] * v_x.

Now p v_y = p * [ - (1+c)(T-2c)/(2s) ] = - p (1+c)(T-2c)/(2s).
